Liability of the Ajir for Negligent Loss: Linguistic and Legal Proof
Linguistic gloss and legal proof for ajir's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the ajir's liability for negligent loss and evaluate the proof based on analogy to a paid deposit.

Core Idea
An ajir is liable for loss he could have prevented because safeguarding is owed by contract, analogous to a paid deposit.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi ijara law: whether a worker (ajir) who receives an item to work on is liable if the item is lost. The passage provides both a linguistic clarification of the term 'hataf anfihi' (natural/unpreventable death) and the legal proof for the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who hold the worker liable for preventable losses like theft. We will unpack both the lexical and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish unavoidable vs. preventable loss
Start by understanding the term 'hataf anfihi' (natural death) and its legal extension. This sets the boundary: losses that are truly unpreventable (like natural death) exempt the worker from liability, while losses that could have been guarded against (like theft) do not.
2
Identify the legal obligation of safeguarding
Recognize that the contract of ijara implies an obligation to safeguard the item. The text states that safeguarding is 'owed' because the worker cannot perform the work without the item, and the contract requires the item to be returned intact.
The phrase 'الحفظ مستحق عليه' (safeguarding is owed) is the key.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) to a paid deposit
The proof draws an analogy to a paid deposit (wadee'a bi-ujr). In a paid deposit, the depositary is liable for loss due to negligence because safeguarding is owed. Similarly, the worker is liable for loss due to preventable causes like theft because he owes safeguarding.
The illa (effective cause) is the obligation to safeguard.
4
Apply the ruling to concrete examples
Test the rule with scenarios: theft (liable), fire not due to worker's fault (not liable if truly unpreventable, but liable if negligence caused fire), natural death of animal (not liable). This reinforces the boundary between preventable and unpreventable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : حتف أنفه ؛ الحتف : الهلاك، قاله ابن فارس، وتبعَه الجَوْهَرِي، ولا يبتنى منه فعل ، يقال : مات حتف أنفه إذا مات من غير ضرب ولا قتل، وزاد الصغاني : ولا حرق ولا غرق. وقال الأَزْهَرِيُّ : لم أسمع للحتف فعلاً ، وحكاه ابن القوطية، فقال: حتفه الله ، يحتفه حتفًا من باب ضرب : إذا أماته ونقل العدل مقبول، ومعناه : أن يموت على فراشه، فيتنفس حتى ينقضي رمقه ؛ ولهذا خص الأنف. ومنه يقال للسمك : يموت في الماء ويطفو : مات حتف أنفه، وهذه الكلمة تكلّم بها أهل الجاهليّة، قال السموأل : وما مات منا سيد حتف أنفه. كذا في «المصباح المنير (1) [٢] قوله : فإن الحفظ...الخ ؛ دليل على مذهبهما، وتقريره : نّ الحفظ مستحق عليه، إذ لا يمكنه العمل إلا به، والعمل السليم من العيب مستحق عليه بالعقد، فإنّ عقد المعاوضة يقتضي سلامة المعقود عليه عن العيب. ومن الظاهر أنّ ما لا يتوصل إلى المستحق إلا به يكون مستحقاً ، فإذا هلك ؟ يمكن الاحتراز . عنه كالغصب ،والسرقة كان التقصير من جهته، فيضمنه كالوديعة إذا كانت بأجر، وكما إذا هلك بفعله، بخلاف ما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه ؛ لأنه لا تقصير من جهته.
[1] His saying: 'hataf anfihi' – al-hataf is destruction, said by Ibn Faris, followed by al-Jawhari, and no verb is derived from it. It is said: He died hataf anfihi if he died without being struck or killed, and al-Saghani added: nor burning nor drowning. And al-Azhari said: I have not heard a verb from hataf, and Ibn al-Quwwiyya narrated it, saying: Hatafahu Allah, yahdifuhu hatafan from the form of daraba: if He caused him to die, and the report of the trustworthy is accepted. Its meaning: that he dies on his bed, breathing until his breath ends; for this reason the nose is specified. From this it is said of a fish: it dies in water and floats: 'He died hataf anfihi'. This word was spoken by the people of Jahiliyya; al-Samaw'al said: None of our masters died hataf anfihi. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir. [2] His saying: 'For indeed safeguarding... etc.' is evidence for their doctrine, and its explanation: That safeguarding is owed by him, because he cannot work except with it, and sound work free from defect is owed by him through the contract. Indeed, a contract of exchange requires the subject matter to be free from defect. And it is obvious that that without which the owed thing cannot be attained is itself owed. So if it perishes in a way that can be guarded against, like usurpation and theft, then there is negligence on his part, so he is liable for it, like a paid deposit, and like if it perishes by his action. Unlike that which cannot be guarded against, because there is no negligence on his part.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage provides two main elements: a linguistic explanation of the term 'hataf anfihi' and the legal proof (dalil) for the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the ajir's liability. First, the commentator clarifies that 'hataf anfihi' means a natural death without external cause, and by extension any unpreventable loss (like fire or drowning). This term appears in the earlier ruling as an exception: if the item is lost in a way similar to 'hataf anfihi' (unpreventable), the worker is not liable. Second, the passage presents the proof for the two imams' doctrine that the worker is liable for negligent loss. Their reasoning runs: the worker is obligated to safeguard the item because he cannot perform the work without it. A contract of exchange (ijara) requires the subject matter (the item) to be free from damage, and safeguarding is necessary to achieve that. Therefore, safeguarding is an owed obligation. If the item is lost due to something preventable like theft or usurpation, that constitutes negligence, and the worker is liable. The proof draws an explicit analogy to a paid deposit (wadee'a bi-ujr), where the depositary is liable because safeguarding is owed. Conversely, if the loss is truly unpreventable (like natural death or general fire), there is no negligence and no liability.

Key Terms
حتف أنفه (hataf anfihi): Natural death without external cause; used metaphorically for unpreventable loss
الوديعة (wadee'a): Deposit; an item given for safekeeping

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what analogy is used to establish the worker's liability for theft?
A) Analogy to a deposit without wages (wadee'a bila ujr).
B) Analogy to a deposit with wages (wadee'a bi-ujr).
C) Analogy to a sale contract.
D) Analogy to a gift contract.

Key Takeaway
An ajir is liable for preventable loss because safeguarding is owed by contract, akin to a paid depositary's duty.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
6 min
Liability of the Ajir for Loss of the Hired Item: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on the liability of the hired person for loss or damage of the hired item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ajir's liability for theft of the hired item and explain the basis of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The ajir is not liable for theft if not negligent – wages are for work, not guarding.

In Hanafi fiqh, the liability of a hired worker for loss or damage to the hired item depends on the nature of the loss. The passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: if the item is stolen without negligence on the ajir's part, is he liable? Abu Hanifa says no; his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), say yes. Understanding this dispute reveals the underlying principles of contract and trust in Hanafi ijara law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: the ajir is not liable for theft if he was not negligent. This is based on the principle that the wages are only for the work, not for guarding the item.
The item is held as an amanah (trust), so guarantee only arises from transgression or contract, neither of which is present.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the ajir is liable even if not negligent, because guarding the item is a required means to perform the work, and thus is owed.
They analogize to a wage-based deposit (wadiah bi-ajr), where the depositary is liable.
3
Analyze the proofs
Examine the dalil of each side. The Sahibayn argue that since guarding is necessary, it is implicitly part of the contract. Abu Hanifa responds that guarding is only a subsidiary duty (taba'an), not independently compensated, so it does not convert the amanah into a guarantee.
The text emphasizes that the primary object of the contract is the work, not the item.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, if the item is stolen and the ajir took reasonable care, he is not liable. Under the Sahibayn, he would be liable regardless of care. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view.
Exception: if the ajir's own work causes the loss (e.g., a washerman tears the cloth), he is liable by consensus.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The matn (al-Hidayah) chooses Abu Hanifa's view. Later scholars like those in al-Hidayah also prefer this, except when the contract stipulates guarantee.
The passage notes a difference among later scholars on the effect of stipulating guarantee – some say it makes the ajir liable even at Abu Hanifa, others say it remains void like a deposit.

كتاب الإجارة باب من الإجارة عنه كالموتِ حتف أنفه، والحرقُ الغالب ، أما إذا سرق، والحالُ أنَّه لم يقصر في المحافظة يضمن عندهما ، كما في الوديعة التي تكون بأجر، فإنَّ الحفظ مستحق عليه ومحل الخلاف هو الإجارة الصحيحة لا الفاسدة، فإنّ الإجارة إن كانت فاسدة ولا ضمان بالاتفاق ؛ لأنّ العين حينئذ تكون أمانة ؛ لكون المعقود عليه وهو المنفعة مضمونة بأجر المثل. كما صرحوا به. (2) في «جامع الرموز»(2 : 77).
Chapter of Ijarah: Section on Ijarah. From him like death by natural causes and overwhelming burning. But if it is stolen while he did not fall short in guarding, he is liable according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), as in the case of a deposit for which payment is made, because guarding is required of him. The place of differing is valid Ijarah, not invalid; for if the Ijarah is invalid, there is no liability by agreement, because then the item is a trust, since the contracted thing (the benefit) is guaranteed by the equivalent wage, as they have stated. [1] Saying: 'death by his nose'; death is destruction, said by Ibn Faris, followed by Jawhari; no verb derived from it. It is said: he died a natural death if he died without beating or killing, and Sagdhani added: nor burning nor drowning. Azhari said: I have not heard a verb for 'hataf', and Ibn al-Qutiyyah narrated it and said: 'hatafahu Allah' 'he gave him death' from the pattern of dharaba. So the word means: to die on his bed breathing until his breath ends; for this it is specified with the nose. Hence it is said of fish that die in water and float: died a natural death. This word was used by the people of Jahiliyyah; Samaw'al said: 'None of us died a natural death.' As in al-Misbah al-Munir. [2] Saying: 'For guarding...etc.': Evidence for their view, and its explanation: guarding is required of him, because he cannot work except with it, and sound work free from defect is required of him by contract; for the contract of exchange necessitates soundness of the contracted thing from defect. And it is evident that what cannot be reached except through it is required. So if it perishes by something that he could not guard against, like usurpation and theft, [he is not liable], but if it perishes by something he could guard against like those, then the shortcoming comes from him, so he guarantees it like a deposit for which wages are given, and also if it perishes by his own action. Contrary to what cannot be guarded against, because there is no shortcoming from him. Rather, what perishes by his work, like the pounding of the washer and similar things. And Abu Hanifa says: The wages are in exchange for the work, not for guarding, so it becomes like an unpaid deposit. But if he stipulates [guarantee], according to some scholars: he guarantees according to Abu Hanifa, and according to some: he does not guarantee. And in the Matn he chose this latter, because stipulation of guarantee in deposit is void. But it can be said: if he stipulates guarantee here, it becomes as if the wages are in exchange for both work and guarding, so it differs from deposit without wages. (Rather, what perishes by his work, like the pounding of the washer and similar things, like the slipping of the porter, the breaking of the camel-driver's rope, and the pulling of the sailor.) [1] Statement: 'and Abu Hanifa...etc.': Its summary: The item is a trust in his hand because he received it with the owner's permission to perform work on it, so it is not guaranteed (mamnunah) for him, like deposit and loan (ariyah), because guarantee either becomes obligatory by transgression or by contract, and neither is found. As for transgression, it is because he received with owner's permission. As for contract, it is because it is upon the work, not upon the item; for this reason, if it perishes due to a cause that cannot be guarded against, he does not guarantee it; if it were guaranteed, he would guarantee it as in the case of usurped property. Guarding is required of him as a subsidiary, not as an independent purpose; for this reason no wage is given for it. Contrary to the case if it perishes by his work, because the contract necessitates soundness of the contracted thing, i.e., the work, so if it is not sound, he guarantees. [2] Statement: 'like the slipping of the porter'; a linguistic matter. It is said: the foot slipped, i.e., from the root z-l-q, meaning did not stay firm until its owner fell. It is transitive with alif and shaddah: 'azlaqtuhu' and 'zallaqtuhu' (I made him slip). As in al-Misbah. This is when it is not due to crowding of people, for the damage resulting from his slipping was due to his failing to walk firmly as they stated. (3) Statement: 'the breaking of the camel-driver's rope'; i.e., the cutting of the rope with which the camel-driver ties the load; for the resulting damage was due to his failure to secure the rope. And the sinking of the ship from the sailor's pulling of it; if it sank due to a wave, wind, collision with a mountain, or the like, he is not liable. Qahistani stated this. (1) al-Misbah al-Munir (1:388). (2) In Jami' al-Rumuz (2:77).
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage deals with the liability (daman) of a hired person (ajir) for the hired item. The text from the matn states that the ajir is not liable for losses like natural death or overwhelming fire. The commentary then discusses a dispute: if the item is stolen without any negligence by the ajir, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold him liable, while Abu Hanifa holds him not liable. The Sahibayn analogize to a deposit taken with wages, where guarding is owed. Abu Hanifa argues that the wages are only for the work, not for guarding, so the item is a trust (amanah) like an unpaid deposit. The passage also clarifies that if the ijarah is invalid, there is no liability by consensus because the item is a trust. However, if the ajir's own work causes the loss (e.g., a washerman crushes the cloth, a porter slips, a camel-driver's rope breaks, a sailor pulls causing the ship to sink), then he is liable because the contract requires the work to be sound. The commentary defines technical terms like 'death by natural causes' and explains that guarding is a subsidiary duty, not directly compensated. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the ajir is not liable for loss beyond his control, but is liable for losses caused by his negligence or the nature of his work.

Key Terms
حتف أنف (hataf anf): Natural death, death on one's bed without violence, burning, or drowning; used metaphorically for death without external cause.
أجير (ajir): A hired person, either ajir khas (exclusive for a period) or ajir mushtarak (shared to perform a specific task). Here it refers to one hired for work on a specific item.
أمانة (amanah): A trust: an item held with the owner's permission, not guaranteeing against loss except due to negligence or transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), when is a hired person (ajir) liable for loss of the hired item?
A) For any loss, including theft, even if not negligent
B) Only if the loss is caused by his own work or negligence, not for external causes beyond his control
C) Only if he has stipulated guarantee in the contract
D) He is never liable because the item is always an amanah

Key Takeaway
The ajir is not liable for theft of the hired item if he was not negligent, because the item is a trust and the wages are for work, not guarding.

Liability of a Common Hireling for Self-Caused Damage
Dispute on liability of ajir mushtarak for self-caused damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the liability of a common hireling for damage caused by his own action and explain the proof, the opposing view, and the practical consequence.

Core Idea
A common hireling's contract demands a sound outcome; a damaging action is unauthorized and compensable.

When a hired craftsman damages the property he works on, who bears the loss? This passage presents the Hanafi position that the common hireling (ajir mushtarak) is liable for damage caused by his own action, because the contract implies a sound performance. The opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i holds that the owner's absolute permission covers both defective and sound outcomes. Understanding this dispute sharpens our grasp of contractual intent and liability in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Parties
Determine whose position is being stated and what the ruling is. Abu Hanifa holds the hireling liable; Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold no liability.
2
State the Hanafi Proof
The contract commands a sound action (al-`amal al-salih). A damaging action is not part of the permission; hence it is unauthorized and triggers liability.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli) not textual.
3
State the Opposing Proof
The owner gave absolute permission (e.g., 'beat the cloth') without stipulating soundness. The command includes both defective and sound outcomes, so no liability.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response
The common hireling is paid for a specific result, unlike a volunteer (private hireling). His permission is limited to corrective work, not corruptive actions.
The volunteer is not restricted to sound work to encourage voluntary service.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
When a fuller tears cloth while beating it, Abu Hanifa says he must compensate; Zufar and al-Shafi'i say no compensation. The Hanafi view is the relied-upon position in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

But what is destroyed by his action, like the beating of the fuller and similar things. And Abu Hanifa says: the wage is in exchange for the work, not for the custody, so it becomes like a deposit without wage. However, if he stipulates the guarantee, then according to some scholars: he guarantees according to Abu Hanifa, and according to some: he does not guarantee. In the Matn, this was chosen because stipulating guarantee in a deposit is invalid. But it can be said: if he stipulates guarantee here, it becomes as if the wage is in exchange for both work and custody, so it differs from a deposit which has no wage. But what is destroyed by his action, like the beating of the fuller and similar things, like the slipping of the porter, the breaking of the rope of the muleteer, and the pulling of the boatman. His saying: 'And Abu Hanifa says...' its précis: The property is a trust in his hand because he took possession with the owner's permission to perform work on it, so it is not guaranteed upon him like a deposit and a loan, because guarantee is either due to transgression or by contract, and neither exists. As for transgression: because he took possession with the owner's permission. As for contract: because it pertains to work, not to the property; hence if it perishes due to a cause from which precaution is impossible, he does not guarantee it, and if it were guaranteed he would guarantee it like in the case of usurped property. Custody is required of him as a consequence, not as a primary intent; hence it is not compensated by wage. Contrary to when it perishes by his action, because the contract requires the soundness of the subject matter of the contract, which is the work, so if it is not sound, he guarantees. His saying: 'like the slipping of the porter'; a bodily slip, it is said: 'the foot slipped' meaning it did not stay fixed until its owner fell, and it is transitive with alif and tashdid, so it is said: 'I made him slip' and 'he slipped'. Thus in al-Misbah, and this is if it was not from crowding of people, for the destruction resulting from his slip resulted from his leaving off firmness in walking, as they made explicit. His saying: 'and the breaking of the rope of the muleteer' means the breaking of the rope with which the muleteer ties the load; for the destruction resulting from it resulted from his leaving off securing in tying the rope, and the sinking of the ship from the boatman's pulling it. If it sank from a wave, wind, hitting a mountain, or similar, he does not guarantee, as al-Quhustani stated. This is our view, and according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, he does not guarantee because he acts with the owner's permission. Our proof: what is commanded is the sound action. I say: one should consider the customary amount, as will come in the discussion of the cupper, or a work in which the known amount is not customary. His saying: 'This is our view' and it is also the view of Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and it is narrated from Umar, Ali, Ubaydullah ibn Utba, Shurayh, al-Hasan, and al-Hakam that the meaning of his saying 'what is destroyed by his action' is an action in which he exceeds the proper measure. His saying: 'And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i' in one opinion, and a narration from Ata and Tawus, he does not guarantee in these cases because the owner of the garment commanded him absolutely to beat the cloth and did not add anything indicating soundness, so this command encompasses both defective and sound, so it is not guaranteed, like the eye of the beater (i.e., actual beating) and the private hireling. His saying: 'Our proof is that...' its establishment: the destruction occurred by an unauthorized action because the action included under permission is that included under the contract, and the action included under the contract is the corrective action, not the corruptive one, because the corrective action is the means to the effect occurring on the property from his doing, and it is the actual subject matter of the contract, even if it occurred by someone else's action, the wage is obligatory, so it is not permitted. Contrary to the one appointed for beating (volunteer) or other, for he is a volunteer, and the volunteer is not restricted to the corrective action, lest people refrain from volunteering for fear of liability. His saying: 'I say: it is appropriate to consider the customary...' the author of al-Manh said: There occurred in al-Imadiyya something contrary to what Sadr al-Sharia researched, where he said: 'If he caused destruction by his action, such as tearing by beating or pressing, he guarantees according to our three scholars, contrary to the cupper, phlebotomist, and barber.' End. Meaning, the cupper and similar do not guarantee what perishes by his action if he does not exceed the customary, and its purport is that the common hireling guarantees what perishes by his action whether he exceeds the customary or not. End of what is in al-Manh.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof and a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Zufar/al-Shafi'i concerning the liability of a common hireling (ajir mushtarak) for property that is destroyed by his own action. The commentator (the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya) first presents Abu Hanifa's position: the hireling is not liable for loss unless it is caused by his own action, because the wage is for the work, not for custody. If the hireling stipulates liability, there is disagreement among later scholars. The core ruling is stated: 'But what is destroyed by his action, like the beating of the fuller...' means the hireling is liable. Examples include a cloth-beater tearing the cloth by beating, a porter slipping, a muleteer's rope breaking, or a boatman causing sinking by pulling. The commentator explains that the property is a trust (trust) in the hireling's hand, so he is not liable for accidental loss, but if the loss results from his faulty performance, he guarantees because the contract demands a sound outcome. The Hanafi proof is that the commanded action is the sound action (al-'amal al-salih); an action that causes damage is outside permission. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the owner gave absolute permission for the act, e.g., to beat the cloth, without stipulating soundness, so the hireling should not be liable. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the common hireling (who is paid for a specified result) and the private hireling (who is paid for his time). The passage also discusses the exception of exceeding customary practice, noting that even if the damage occurs without exceeding the customary, the common hireling still guarantees according to the preferred view.

Key Terms
أجير مشترك (ajīr mushtarak): A common hireling who performs work for multiple clients without exclusive dedication of his time (e.g., a fuller, porter).
ضمان (ḍamān): Financial liability to compensate for loss or damage.
عمل صالح (‘amal ṣāliḥ): A sound, permissible, and effective action that fulfills the contract's purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is a common hireling (ajir mushtarak) liable for damage caused by his own action?
A) Because the property is a trust and any damage must be compensated.
B) Because the contract of hire implies a sound action, and a damaging action is outside the permission.
C) Because the hireling is always liable for any loss, regardless of cause.
D) Because the owner explicitly required guarantee in the contract.

Key Takeaway
A common hireling (ajir mushtarak) is liable for damage caused by his own action because the contract implies a sound outcome; a damaging action is unauthorized and compensable.

Liability of the Common Employee for Damage Beyond Normal Work
Dispute about liability of common employee for damage from his work

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the liability of the ajir mushtarak for damage caused by his work and explain the basis for the disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A common employee guarantees damage only when he exceeds customary work, because the contract only authorizes sound work.

When you hire someone to perform a task, is he liable if he damages your property while working? The Hanafi school says it depends on whether his action was customary. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya examines a dispute about the common employee (ajir mushtarak) who tears cloth while beating it. The Hanafi position is that tearing is uncustomary, so he must compensate. But Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the owner's general permission covers both sound and defective outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding the liability of the ajir mushtarak when his work causes damage. The key is whether the work was customary (sound) or uncustomary (destructive).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that the employee does not guarantee. Understand their core argument: absolute permission covers all outcomes.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Trace their dalil: the owner gave a general command (to beat the cloth), and this command includes both sound and defective results, just as in the case of a miller or private employee.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: the contract is specifically for sound work (al-mustaslih). Destructive work is not included in the permission, so the employee acts without authorization and is liable.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi, the employee compensates for damage from uncustomary work; under Zufar/Shafi'i, no compensation even if damage occurs.

Source Text

كتاب الإجارة باب من الإجارة
و (۱) هذا عندنا، وعند زفر والشافعي ( ) " لا يضمن ؛ لأنه يعمل بإذن المالك. ولنا : أن المأمور به العمل الصالح.
أقول (٣) : ينبغي المعتاد على ما يأتي في الحجام، أو عملاً لا يعتاد فيه المقدار المعلوم. [٤] قوله : أقول ينبغي ... الخ ؛ قال صاحب «المنح»: قد وقع في «العمادية» مـا يخالف ما يبحثه صدر الشريعة الله حيث قال : وإن ملك بفعله بأن تخرق بدقه أو عصره، يضمن عند علمائنا الثلاثة لله بخلاف البزاغ والفصاد والحجام. انتهى. يعني فإنَّ البزاغَ ونحوه لا يضمن ما هلك بفعله إذا لم يجاوز المعتاد، ومفاده : إنّ الأجير المشترك يضمن ما هلك بفعلِهِ جاوزَ المعتاد أم لا. انتهى ما في «المنح».

Book of Ijarah, chapter on Ijarah. And (1) This is with us, and with Zufar and al-Shafi'i: "He does not guarantee because he acts with the permission of the owner." And for us: that what is commanded is sound work. I say (3): It should be customary as will come regarding the cupper, or work in which the known amount is not customary. [1] His statement: This is with us; and Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i in one view said it, and it is narrated from Umar, Ali, Ubaydullah ibn Utbah, Shurayh, al-Hasan, and al-Hakam that what is meant by his statement: what is lost by his work, he has exceeded the proper measure. [2] His statement: With Zufar and al-Shafi'i, in one view, and a narration from Ata and Tawus, he does not guarantee in these cases because the owner of the garment ordered him to act absolutely by hiring him to beat the cloth and did not add anything indicating safety, so this command includes the defective and the sound, so it is not guaranteed like the case of the miller and the private employee. [3] His statement: And for us indeed... etc.; its explanation: That the loss occurred by unauthorized work because the work included under permission is that which is included under the contract, and that included under the contract is the work that is reformative, not corruptive, because reformative work is the means to the effect produced in the object by his action, and it is what is contracted for in reality, such that if it occurred by another's action, the wage is obligatory, so it would not be permitted, unlike the case of the dedicated miller or other, for he is a volunteer, and a volunteer is not restricted to reformative work, lest people refrain from volunteering for fear of liability. [4] His statement: I say it should be... etc.; the author of al-Manh said: There has occurred in al-Imadiyya something that contradicts what Sadr al-Shariah researched, where he said: If he destroys by his action, such as tearing by beating or pressing, he guarantees according to our three imams, unlike the brander, phlebotomist, and cupper. End. Meaning that the brander and the like do not guarantee what perishes by his action if he does not exceed the customary, and its purport is that the common employee guarantees what perishes by his action whether he exceeds the customary or not. End from al-Manh. And al-Quhustani strengthened the statement of the excellent commentator where he said: Rather he guarantees by his action what perishes of animal or other by his action unauthorized work like the beating that tears the cloth as in al-Muhit and others, so it is necessarily uncustomary; and therefore the author interpreted the work by it, so it is false what one thought that the author's interpretation of it according to what is in al-Kafi is invalid: Indeed, the strength and thinness of the cloth, for example, is known by exertion, so it is possible to restrict it to what is sound. End. And Allama al-Shami said in Radd al-Muhtar: The implication of his words is that every destructive work is uncustomary, so the restriction of Sadr al-Shariah of what is lost by his work to his statement: uncustomary work is not valid, and it remains contradicting what is in al-Kafi which gives the benefit that destructive work may be customary. This, and what appears to me is that there is no contradiction between their statements, and that all say that what destroys the cloth is uncustomary. But because for the cupper his guarantee is restricted to uncustomary, not customary, they intended to indicate that for the fuller it is not restricted by this restriction, to show the difference between them. But departure from the customary in the case of cloth does not appear to us except through destruction, so wherever it is destructive, we know it is uncustomary, so he guarantees due to his negligence. For the skilled in his craft perceives the destruction, unlike the cupper, for his work is in a specific place, so if he does not exceed it, he does not guarantee, because he cannot perceive it by his skill, so guarantee is tied to exceeding the specific place. Thus it appears that every destruction in the work of the fuller is outside the customary, as indicated by what is in al-Badai, which is that he can avoid by exertion through looking at the tool and its place, and releasing the mallet on the spot to the extent it can bear with skill in work, and when these conditions are observed, damage does not occur; so when it does occur, it indicates that he was negligent, and in the rights of people it is not an excuse. End. So it is known that there is no difference between the two statements, even if there is looseness in expression, so understand. End from Radd al-Muhtar. And Allah knows best the reality of secrets.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on a dispute regarding the liability of a common employee (ajir mushtarak) when his work causes damage. The core issue: if an employee hired to perform a specific task (like beating cloth) causes the cloth to tear, is he liable? The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that he is liable if the work that caused damage was not authorized. The work authorized is sound work (al-mustaslih), not corruptive work. Therefore, if he exceeds the customary practice and causes damage, he has acted without permission and must compensate. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that he is not liable because the owner gave absolute permission for the work, and the command includes both sound and defective outcomes, analogous to the miller or private employee. The commentary then reconciles various statements: the cupper (hajjam) is only liable if he exceeds the customary site; the fuller (qassar) is liable whenever destruction occurs because destruction itself indicates departure from custom, as he could have prevented it with skill. The author of Radd al-Muhtar explains that there is no real contradiction: for the fuller, the very fact of destruction shows uncustomary action, while for the cupper, the customary site is known, so exceeding that is the criterion. Thus, the common employee is always liable if his work causes damage beyond the customary, as that indicates negligence.

Key Terms
الأجير المشترك (al-ajir al-mushtarak): A hired worker who works for multiple people or whose work is not exclusively for one employer; a common employee.
العمل المصلح (al-amal al-muslih): Work that is sound and beneficial, not causing damage; the proper object of the contract.
المعتاد (al-mu'tad): What is customary or usual in the trade; exceeding it makes the work unauthorized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the common employee (ajir mushtarak) guarantee damage when he tears the cloth by beating it?
A) Because he was negligent in his work.
B) Because the work that caused the tear was not authorized under the contract.
C) Because the owner did not specify the quality of the cloth.
D) Because he is a volunteer and thus liable for any damage.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a common employee compensates for damage if his work was not customary, because the contract only permits sound work.

Washerman's Liability for Non-Customary Damage
Reconciling views on liability for non-customary acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between customary and non-customary acts of artisans and state when liability arises according to the Hanafi reconciliation.

Core Idea
A washerman is liable for damage because it proves negligence; a cupper is only liable if he exceeds the norm.

In this passage, the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah resolves an apparent contradiction between earlier Hanafi texts regarding when an artisan is liable for damage. Some sources seemed to say that every destructive act is non-customary and thus triggers liability, while others implied that even destructive acts can be customary and excusable. The key to reconciliation lies in the nature of the work: a washerman can gauge the cloth's strength through skill, so any tearing shows negligence; a cupper cannot gauge the patient's internal state, so he is only liable if he exceeds the customary limit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Note that one set of texts (e.g., Al-Kafi) suggests that a destructive act may still be customary, while another (e.g., Al-Muhit) implies all destructive acts are non-customary. The author of the passage shows that these are not actually contradictory.
2
Analyze the distinction between washerman and cupper
The passage explains that a washerman uses ijtihad (skill) to judge the cloth's strength, so if damage occurs, it indicates negligence (taqsir). A cupper works on a fixed location and cannot foresee internal factors, so he is only liable if he exceeds the usual place or depth.
This distinction is based on the possibility of avoidance through skill.
3
Understand the role of ijtihad (skill)
In this context, ijtihad means the artisan's professional judgment. If the damage could have been avoided through proper skill, the act is considered non-customary, and liability follows.
Compare with the cupper: no amount of skill can predict the patient's internal condition.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Generalize: any artisan whose work involves judgment of material properties (e.g., tailor, blacksmith) is liable for damage that a skilled peer could avoid. Artisans working on live subjects (e.g., barber, surgeon) are only liable for exceeding conventional bounds.
This is the operative rule for determining 'customary' acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوى القُهستاني : قول الشارح البارع لله حيث قال : بل يضمن بعمله ما هلك من حيوان وغيره بعمله عملاً غير مأذون فيه كالدقّ المخرق للثوب كما في «المحيط» وغيره ، فهو غير معتاد بالضرورة ؛ ولذا فسَّر المصنِّفُ العمل به، فمن الباطل ما ظن أنه بطل تفسير المصنف له بما في «الكافي»: إنّ قوَّة الثوب ورقته مثلاً يعرف بالاجتهاد، فأمكن التقييد بالصلح. انتهى. وقال العلامة الشامي في رد المحتار : ومقتضى كلامه أن كل عمل متلف يكون غير معتاد، فلا يصح تقييد صدر الشريعة الله له ما تلف بعمله لقوله : عملاً غير معتاد، ويبقى مخالفاً لما في «الكافي» المفيدُ أنّ العمل المتلف قد يكون معتاداً، هذا والذي يظهر لي أنه لا منافاة بين كلامهم، وأنّ الكلّ يقولون : إن المتلف للثوب غير معتاد. ولكن لما كان نحو الحجام ضمانه مقيد بغير المعتاد دون المعتاد، أرادوا التنبيه على أن نحو القصار غير مقيد بهذا القيد ؛ ليفيدوا الفرق بينهما ، ولكن الخروج عن المعتاد في نحو الثوب لا يظهر لنا إلا بالإتلاف، فحيث كان متلفاً علم أنه غير معتاد، فيضمن لتقصيره. فإن الماهر في صنعه يدركُ المتلف بخلاف نحو الحجام، فإن بعمله محلاً مخصوصاً، فإذا لم يجاوزه لا يضمن، فإنّه لا يمكن إدراكه بمهارته ، فأنيط الضمان على مجاوزته المحلّ المخصوص. فظهر بهذا أن كل متلف في عمل نحو القصار خارج عن المعتاد يدل عليه ما في «البدائع»، وهو أنه يمكنه التحرّزُ بالاجتهاد بالنظر في آلة الدق ومحله، وإرسال المدقة على المحلّ على قدر ما يحتمله مع الحذاقة في العمل، وعند مراعاة هذه الشرائط لا يحصل الفساد، فلما حصل دلَّ على أنه مقصر ، وهو في حقوق العباد ليس بعذر. انتهى. فعُلِمَ أنه لا فرق بين الكلامين، وإن كان في التعبير مسامحة فافهم. انتهى ما في «رد المحتار)، والله أعلم بحقيقة الأسرار.
And Qahistani strengthened (it): The saying of the excellent commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) where he said: Rather, he is liable by his action for whatever is destroyed of animals and other things by his action, an unauthorized action, like the beating that tears the cloth, as in Al-Muhit and others. So it is necessarily non-customary; therefore the author interpreted the action by it. So it is false what was thought that the author's interpretation of it was invalidated by what is in Al-Kafi: Indeed, the strength and thinness of the cloth, for example, is known by ijtihad (estimation), so it was possible to restrict it to (damage from) sound (cloth). End. And Allamah al-Shami said in Radd al-Muhtar: The implication of his speech is that every destructive act is non-customary, so it is not correct to restrict (the liability of) Sadr al-Shari'ah (may Allah have mercy on him) to what is destroyed by his action because of his saying: 'a non-customary action,' and it remains contradictory to what is in Al-Kafi which states that the destructive action may be customary. This, and what appears to me is that there is no contradiction between their statements, and that all say that the destroyer of the cloth is non-customary. But since the liability of a cupper, for example, is restricted to the non-customary and not the customary, they intended to indicate that a washerman, for example, is not restricted by this restriction; to establish the difference between them. However, the departure from the customary in the case of cloth does not appear to us except by destruction, so wherever it is destructive, it is known to be non-customary, so he is liable due to his negligence. For the skilled artisan can perceive the destructive (nature) unlike the cupper, for his action is in a specific place, so if he does not exceed it, he is not liable, because it cannot be perceived by his skill. So liability is tied to his exceeding the specific place. Thus it appears that every destroyer in the work of a washerman, for example, is outside the customary, as indicated by what is in Al-Bada'i: that he can avoid (damage) by ijtihad (skill) by considering the beating tool and its place, and sending the mallet onto the place according to what it can bear with skill in the work, and when these conditions are observed, corruption does not occur. So when it occurs, it indicates that he is negligent, and that is no excuse in the rights of people. End. So it is known that there is no difference between the two statements, even if there is looseness in expression, so understand. End of what is in Radd al-Muhtar. And Allah knows best the reality of secrets.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) from the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, reconciling apparent contradictions between different Hanafi sources regarding the liability of artisans like washermen. The core issue is whether every act that causes damage is considered 'non-customary' (ghayr mu'tad) and thus triggers liability, or whether some acts can be customary yet still cause damage. The author, citing al-Qahistani and then al-Shami, resolves this by distinguishing between artisans whose work inherently involves risk of damage (like washermen beating cloth) and those whose work is on a specific body part (like cupping). For a washerman, because he can judge the cloth's strength through skill, any damage indicates he acted negligently, so his act is deemed non-customary and he is liable. For a cupper, the outcome depends on factors beyond his skill (like the patient's constitution), so he is only liable if he exceeds the customary bounds (e.g., cuts deeper than usual). Thus, there is no real contradiction: both statements agree that liability arises from non-customary acts, but what constitutes 'non-customary' differs based on the nature of the work.

Key Terms
غير معتاد (ghayr mu'tad): In this passage, an act that departs from the customary practice of the skilled artisan, evidenced by causing damage that could have been avoided with proper skill.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Here, the skilled judgment of the artisan in estimating the strength of the material and applying appropriate force.
التقصير (al-taqsir): Negligence in performing the work, failing to exercise the required care that a skilled artisan would normally exert.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation in this passage, why is a washerman liable for tearing a cloth through beating, while a cupper is not liable if he does not exceed the customary limit?
A) Because washing is a more dangerous profession than cupping.
B) Because a washerman can judge the cloth's strength through skill, so damage indicates negligence; a cupper cannot judge the patient's internal condition, so only exceeding the norm is negligence.
C) Because the cupper's act is always considered customary, while the washerman's is always non-customary.
D) Because the washerman's contract includes a guarantee of safety, unlike the cupper's contract.

Key Takeaway
The classification of an artisan's act as customary or non-customary depends on whether damage could have been avoided through proper skill, which determines liability.

Non-Liability for Human Injury in Ijarah: The Cupper's Exception
Explaining non-liability for human injury and limits of cupper's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a contracted carrier is not liable for accidental human injury, state the rule for artisans (cupper, phlebotomist) who do not exceed customary practice, and identify when liability arises due to transgression.

Core Idea
Humans are not guaranteed by contract; liability for harm is by tort only, and artisans are exempt unless they transgress custom.

In Hanafi fiqh, a fundamental distinction exists between contractual liability (damān al-'aqd) and tort liability (damān al-jināyah). This passages from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah tackles two practical scenarios: a carrier whose actions cause a human to drown or fall, and an artisan (cupper, phlebotomist, farrier) who causes harm during his work. The rulings hinge on whether the harm resulted from a transgression (ta'addī) or was within customary bounds. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying liability rules in hire contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and their rulings
The passage first addresses a carrier: a human drowns due to ship pulling or falls from an animal due to saddle tightening. The ruling is non-liability. Then it addresses artisans: cupper, farrier, phlebotomist – they are also not liable if they do not exceed customary practice.
Note the conditional for artisans: 'lam yujāwiz al-mu'tād' (did not exceed the customary).
2
Extract the general principle
The commentary states: 'because the human is not guaranteed by contract, but rather by crime (jināyah).' This is the core principle – human life and limb are not objects of contractual guarantee (damān al-'aqd).
Contrast with goods (amwāl) which are guaranteed by contract.
3
Apply the principle to the carrier case
Since the carrier’s contract is about goods, not humans, any injury to a human is outside the contractual framework. Liability only arises if there is ta'addī (transgression), which is assumed absent in the default scenario.
The sub-issue about the person falling off an animal: the correct view is that it makes no difference whether he holds on firmly or not – non-liability applies regardless.
4
Apply the principle to the artisan case
For artisans, the act is obligatory under the contract. An obligatory act cannot coexist with liability unless the harm could have been avoided. Since the artisan cannot fully control internal factors (e.g., patient's constitution), liability is only triggered if he exceeds the customary limit (mu'tād).
The analogy with a cloth beater is given: cloth strength can be judged, so liability may arise if he beats too hard. For phlebotomy, such judgment is impossible, so only exceeding custom creates liability.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on the falling person
There is a reported view that liability exists if the person does not hold on firmly, but the sahīh (correct) view is that there is no difference – both cases are non-liable. This is settled by al-Tumurtāshī and supported by Ibn Samā'ah's narration from Abu Yusuf.
The author affirms the sahīh view, which is the muftā bihī position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة باب من الإجارة
(ولا يضمن به آدمياً غَرِق ، أو سقط من دابة) : أي آدميا غرق بسبب مد السفينة، أو سقط من الدابة بسبب شد المكارى ؛ لأنَّ الآدمي غير مضمون بالعقد، بل بالجناية، وضمان العقود لا يتحمله العاقلة، ولا حجام، أو بزاغ، أو فصاد لم يجاوز المعتاد.
قوله : أو سقط من دابة ؛ قيل : إنَّما عدم الضمان حال السقوط إذا كان هو تمن يستمسك على الدابة ويركب وحده، وإلا فهو كالمتاع، والصحيح أنه لا فرق، وكذا رواه ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف الله في الوضع. كذا ذكره التمرتاشي . ولا يقال : إن ضمان بني آدم يجب بالتسبيب ، وقد وجد ؛ لأنَّ المسبب إنما يضمن إذا تعدى ، وكلامنا فيما إذا لم يوجد التعدي. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله : لأن الآدمي...الخ ؛ أي لأنَّ الآدمي لا يجب ضمانه بالعقـد، بـل يجب بالجناية، ومـا يجـب بـهـا يجب على العاقلة، والعاقلة لا تتحمل ضمان العقود ؛ لأنَّ العاقلة إنما يدفعون الدِّية باعتبار تركِ الحفظ، ولا يجب عليهم الحفظ من العقود، فإذا كان كذلك لا تتحمّل ضمان العقود، وهذا ليس بجناية ; لكونه مأذوناً فيه.
قوله : ولا حجام أو بزاغ..الخ ؛ أي ولا يضمن حجام أو بزاغ أو فصاد لم يجاوز المعتاد ; لأنه التزمه بالعقد فصار واجباً عليه، والفعلُ الواجب لا يجامعه الضمان، كما إذا حد القاضي أو غزر ومات المضروب بذلك، إلا إذا كان يمكنه التحرُّزُ عنه كدق الثوب ونحوه مما ذكرنا من قبل ; لأنَّ قوة الثوب ورقته يعرفُ به ما يتحمله من الدق بالاجتهاد، فأمكن تقييده بالسليم منه. بخلاف الفصد ونحوه، فإنّه يبتني على قوة الطبائع وضعفها، ولا يعرف ذلك بنفسه، ولا ما يتحمله من الجرح، فلا يمكن تقييده بالسليم، وهو غير الساري، فسقط اعتباره إلا إذا جاوز المعتاد فيضمن الزائد كله إذا لم يهلك.
Book of Ijarah, Chapter of Ijarah
(And he is not liable by it for a human who drowns, or falls from an animal): i.e., a human who drowns due to the pulling of the ship, or falls from the animal due to the tightening of the pack‑saddle ; because the human is not guaranteed by contract, but rather by crime, and the 'aqilah does not bear the guarantee of contracts. And (he is not liable for) a cupper, or a farrier, or a phlebotomist who does not exceed the customary.
His saying: 'or falls from an animal' – it is said: Indeed, the non‑liability at the time of falling is only if he is such that he holds onto the animal and rides alone; otherwise he is like goods, but the correct view is that there is no difference, and likewise Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Abu Yusuf in al‑Wad' (al‑Waqi'at). Thus mentioned al‑Tumurtashi. And it cannot be said that the guarantee of humans is obligatory due to causation, and it has occurred; because the causer is only liable if he transgresses, and our discussion is where transgression does not occur. Thus in al‑Kifayah.
His saying: 'because the human...' etc.; i.e., because the human's guarantee is not obligatory by contract, but is obligatory by crime, and what is obligatory by it is upon the 'aqilah, and the 'aqilah do not bear the guarantee of contracts; because the 'aqilah only pay the blood‑money due to their failure to protect, and they are not obligated to protect from contracts, so when this is the case, they do not bear the guarantee of contracts, and this is not a crime because it is permitted.
His saying: 'nor a cupper or a farrier...' etc.; i.e., a cupper, farrier, or phlebotomist who does not exceed the customary is not liable; because he undertook it by contract so it became obligatory upon him, and an obligatory act is not combined with liability, just as when a judge flogs or a cupper cups and the struck person dies thereby, except if he could have avoided it like the beating of cloth and similar to what we mentioned before; because the strength and thinness of the cloth are known such that what it can bear of beating can be known by ijtihad, so it was possible to restrict it to sound (cloth). Unlike phlebotomy and the like, for it depends on the strength and weakness of natures, and he does not know that by himself, nor does he know what the wound can bear, so it is not possible to restrict it to sound (condition), and it is not (like) the spreading (of damage), so it (the restriction) falls away. But if he exceeds the customary, then he is liable for the entire excess if (the patient) does not die.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on a matn from the Book of Ijarah. It states two distinct rulings: (1) A person contracted to carry goods is not liable if a human drowns due to his ship or falls from his animal, because liability for human life is not contractual but tort‑based (jinayah). The 'aqilah (tribal guarantors) bear blood‑money for tort, but they do not bear contractual guarantees. (2) A cupper (hajjam), farrier (bazagh), or phlebotomist (fassad) is not liable for harm as long as he does not exceed the customary practice, because his act is obligatory under the contract. An obligatory act does not incur liability unless he could have avoided the harm through skill, like a washerman who can judge cloth strength. But for phlebotomy, the outcome depends on the patient's internal constitution which the practitioner cannot gauge, so he is only liable if he goes beyond the customary (e.g., cuts too deep). The passage also clarifies a sub‑issue: the non‑liability for a person falling from an animal applies regardless of whether he holds on firmly or not, contrary to a weaker view.

Key Terms
آدمي (adami): A human being, as opposed to property (mal). In this context, it refers to a free human whose life is not subject to contractual compensation.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The paternal relatives (asabah) who bear the blood‑money (diyah) for unintentional homicide or injury caused by one of them. They do not bear contractual liabilities.
المعتاد (al-mu'tad): The customary practice of the profession; what is normally done by skilled practitioners without harm. For cupping, this includes typical incision depth and location.
الجناية (al-jinayah): Criminal or tortious act causing harm to a person. Liability for such acts is borne by the 'aqilah for unintentional harm, and by the perpetrator for intentional harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a cupper not liable for harm to a patient if he does not exceed the customary practice, even if harm occurs?
A) Because cupping is a noble profession exempt from liability.
B) Because the cupper's act is obligatory under the contract, and an obligatory act cannot be combined with liability unless he could have avoided the harm through skill.
C) Because the patient signed a waiver.
D) Because the cupper is considered an agent of the state.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, liability for human injury is not contractual but tort-based; artisans are exempt from liability when acting within customary bounds because their act is obligatory and the risk is unknowable.

No Liability for Persons in Ijarah: Non-Contractual Liability
Clarifying that the hirer is not liable for persons in ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a hirer is not liable for persons in ijarah, explain the proof using the distinction between contractual and tort liability, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute and the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A person is not guaranteed by contract; liability requires transgression.

When a person drowns because of a boat's movement or falls from an animal during a hire contract, one might think the hirer is liable. However, the Hanafi ruling is clear: humans are not covered by contractual guarantee. This lesson explores the legal reasoning behind this, focusing on the distinction between liability by contract and liability by transgression, and addresses a dispute within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruled Act and the Ruling
What specific action is being ruled on? (e.g., a porter stretching a boat causing drowning) and what is the ruling? (non-liability for persons).
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
What is the rational argument (dalil aqli) given? The text states: 'Liability for a person is not established by contract, but only by transgression.' And 'the act is permitted, so no transgression.'
This is a case of istidlal bi al-aql.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How exactly does the principle 'liability by contract does not apply to persons' lead to non-liability? Because the contract (ijarah) is a permission to act, and since the act is permitted, no transgression (jinayah) exists.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Some scholars said if the person does not hold on, he is like goods (liable). How does the text resolve this? It says the correct view is no distinction, supported by transmission from Abu Yusuf.
5
Refute the Objection Based on Causation
An objector might say causation (tasbib) alone creates liability. The response: causation only creates liability if there is transgression, which is absent here.
This clarifies the limit of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا يضمن به آدمياً غَرِق ، أو سقط من دابة) : أي آدميا غرق بسبب مد السفينة، أو سقط من الدابة بسبب شد المكارى ؛ لأنَّ الآدمي غير مضمون بالعقد، بل بالجناية، وضمان العقود لا يتحمله العاقلة. [ ١ ] قوله : أو سقط من دابة ؛ قيل : إنَّما عدم الضمان حال السقوط إذا كان هو تمن يستمسك على الدابة ويركب وحده، وإلا فهو كالمتاع، والصحيح أنه لا فرق، وكذا رواه ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف الله في الوضع. كذا ذكره التمرتاشي . ولا يقال : إن ضمان بني آدم يجب بالتسبيب ، وقد وجد ؛ لأنَّ المسبب إنما يضمن إذا تعدى ، وكلامنا فيما إذا لم يوجد التعدي. كذا في «الكفاية». [٢] قوله : لأن الآدمي...الخ ; أي لأنَّ الآدمي لا يجب ضمانه بالعقـد، بـل يجب بالجناية، ومـا يجـب بـهـا يجب على العاقلة، والعاقلة لا تتحمل ضمان العقود ; لأنَّ العاقلة إنما يدفعون الدِّية باعتبار تركِ الحفظ، ولا يجب عليهم الحفظ من العقود، فإذا كان كذلك لا تتحمّل ضمان العقود، وهذا ليس بجناية ; لكونه مأذوناً فيه.
(And he does not become liable by it for a person who drowns, or falls from an animal): i.e., a person who drowns due to the stretching of the boat, or falls from the animal due to the tightening of the saddle, because the human is not guaranteed by contract, but only by transgression, and the guarantee of contracts is not borne by the 'aqila. [1] His saying: 'or falls from an animal'; it is said: the absence of liability at the time of falling is if he is one who clings to the animal and rides alone, otherwise he is like goods, and the correct view is that there is no difference. Similarly, it was narrated by Ibn Sama'a from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) in al-Wadi'. Thus mentioned by al-Tamartashi. And let it not be said that liability for human beings is established by causation, and it has occurred; because the one who causes liability is only liable if he transgresses, and we are speaking about when transgression does not occur. Thus in al-Kifaya. [2] His saying: 'because the human...' etc.; i.e., because liability for the human is not obligatory by contract, but rather by transgression, and what is obligatory by it is obligatory upon the 'aqila, and the 'aqila does not bear the liability of contracts; because the 'aqila only pays the blood money on account of neglect of protection, and they are not obliged to protect from contracts. Since this is the case, they do not bear the liability of contracts, and this is not a transgression because it is permitted.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that the hirer (or carrier) is not liable for the drowning of a person caused by stretching the boat, or for a person falling from an animal due to tightening the saddle. The reason is that liability for persons (آدمي) is not established by contract (عقد) but only by transgression (جناية). Liability under contracts is not borne by the 'aqila (tribal insurance). The 'aqila only pays blood money due to negligence in custody, and they are not required to guard against contract obligations. Since the action is permitted (مأذون فيه), it is not a transgression. There is a discussion about whether there is a distinction between the person holding on or not; the correct view is no distinction. The text also refutes an objection that liability could arise from causation (تسبيب); the response is that causation only incurs liability if there is transgression, which is absent here.

Key Terms
آدمي (adamī): A human being, as opposed to property.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability or guarantee, specifically financial responsibility.
جناية (jināyah): A transgression or tortious act, especially against the person.
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The collective of male relatives who pay blood money (diya) on behalf of a killer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because liability requires transgression, which is absent.
D) Because the 'aqila pays.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, a hirer is not liable for harm to persons unless transgression occurs; contractual guarantee does not cover human lives.

Liability of Medical Practitioners: Customary Limits and the Circumciser Exception
Explaining non-liability of medical practitioners within customary limits and the rare circumciser case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of non-liability for medical practitioners within customary limits and apply the exceptional circumciser ruling to determine diya amounts.

Core Idea
An obligatory act negates liability; the circumciser's diya is full upon recovery, half upon death due to shared causation.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition addresses a critical question: when is a medical professional liable for harm caused during treatment? The general rule is that if the practitioner stays within the customary bounds of the procedure, there is no liability because the act is obligatory by contract. However, an exceptional case arises for the circumciser: cutting the glans triggers full diya if the patient recovers, but only half diya if the patient dies. This lesson will unpack the legal reasoning behind both principles.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between the general rule and the exception
Identify that the first part of the passage covers cupping, veterinary, and phlebotomy, and the second part covers circumcision. Note that the general rule is 'no liability within custom', while the circumciser has a special rule.
2
Understand the rationale for non-liability of medical practitioners
Recognize that the act is obligatory (wajib) due to the contract, and a wajib act does not coexist with liability (daman). Compare to a judge who administers punishment—if death results, no liability unless excessive.
3
Identify the condition for liability: exceeding customary limits
Note that if the practitioner exceeds the customary norm (e.g., cuts deeper than usual), liability arises for the excess harm. The text distinguishes between measurable precautions (like cloth beating) and immeasurable internal factors (like phlebotomy).
4
Analyze the circumciser case and its reasoning
Study the ruling: if the glans is cut and the patient recovers, full diya is due; if the patient dies, half diya. Understand the proof from Imam Muhammad: the glans is a complete organ with no equal, so its diya equals the full diya of a person. When death occurs, it is caused by two acts (permitted and unpermitted), splitting liability.
This is an exception from al-Nawadir; it is rare (gharib) but accepted.
5
Address the objection and its rebuttal
Consider the objection: this ruling contradicts the pattern where greater harm leads to less liability. The rebuttal: the glans cut is not inevitably fatal like a neck incision; both acts are potentially fatal, so the difference is not measurable, hence the half-and-half division is fair.
The rebuttal uses the principle of non-measurability (ghayr maddabitt).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) قوله : ولا حجام أو بزاغ..الخ ; أي ولا يضمن حجام أو بزاغ أو فصاد لم يجاوز المعتاد ; لأنه التزمه بالعقد فصار واجباً عليه، والفعلُ الواجب لا يجامعه الضمان ، كما إذا حد القاضي أو غزر ومات المضروب بذلك، إلا إذا كان يمكنه التحرُّزُ عنه كدق الثوب ونحوه مما ذكرنا من قبل ; لأنَّ قوة الثوب ورقته يعرفُ به ما يتحمله من الدق بالاجتهاد، فأمكن تقييده بالسليم منه. بخلاف الفصد ونحوه ، فإنّه يبتني على قوة الطبائع وضعفها، ولا يعرف ذلك بنفسه، ولا ما يتحمله من الجرح ، فلا يمكن تقييده بالسليم، وهو غير الساري، فسقط اعتباره إلا إذا جاوز المعتاد فيضمن الزائد كله إذا لم يهلك. (۱) بزاغ : أي بيطار، هو خاص بالبهائم. ينظر : رد المحتار»(٥ : ٤٣). (۲) «الكفاية (٨ : ٦٦ - ٦٧). وإن هلك يضمن نصف ديةِ النفس ; لأنها تلفت بمأذون فيه، وغير مأذون فيه فيضمن بحسابه، وهو النصف، حتى أنّ الختَّانَ لو قطع الحشفة وبرأ المقطوع تجب عليه دية كاملة ; لأنَّ الزائد هو الحشفة، وهو عضو كامل، فيجب عليه الدية كاملة، وإن مات وجب عليه نصف الدية لما ذكرنا، وهي من أندر المسائل وأغربها، حيث تجب الأكثر بالبراء، وبالهلاكِ أقلّ. كذا في «التبيين» فإن قيل : هذا مخالف لجميع مسائل الديات، فإنّه كلما ازداد أثر جنايتها نقص ضمانه. أجيب : بأن محمداً له قال في «النوادر» : وإنه لما برئ كان عليه ضمان الحشفة ، وهي عضو مقصود لا ثاني له في النفس، فيتقدَّر بدله ببدل النفس كما في قطع اللسان، وأما إذا مات فقد حصل تلف النفس بفعلين، أحدهما مأذون فيه وهو قطع الجلدة، والآخر غير مأذون فيه وهو قطع الحشفة، فكان ضامناً نصف بدل النفس. فإن قيل : التنصيفُ في البدل يعتمد التساوي في السبب، وقد انتفى ; لأنَّ قطعَ الحشفة أشدّ إفضاء إلى التلف من قطع الجلدة لا محالة، فكان كقطع اليد مع حز الرقبة. أجيب : بأن كل واحدٍ يحتمل أن يقع إتلافاً وأن لا يقع، والتفاوت غير مضبوط ، بخلاف الحز فإنّه لا يحتمل أن لا يقع إتلافاً. كذا في «العناية».
(3) His saying: 'and no cupper, or veterinarian, or phlebotomist...' etc.; i.e., a cupper, veterinarian, or phlebotomist does not become liable if he does not exceed the customary, because he undertook it by contract so it became obligatory upon him, and an obligatory act does not coexist with liability, just as when a judge administers flogging or fixed punishment and the one struck dies thereby, except if he could have avoided it like beating cloth and similar from what we mentioned before; because the strength and thinness of cloth can be known regarding what it can bear from beating by ijtihad, so it is possible to restrict it to sound cloth. Unlike phlebotomy and similar, which depends on the strength and weakness of natures, and he does not know that by himself, nor what it can bear from the wound, so it is not possible to restrict it to sound condition, and it is not spreading; so its consideration falls away, unless he exceeds the customary, and then he guarantees the entire excess if he does not cause death. (1) Bazzagh: i.e., veterinarian; it is specific to animals. See: Radd al-Muhtar (5:43). (2) Al-Kifaya (8:66-67). And if he causes death, he guarantees half the blood money of the person, because the death occurred by a permitted act and a non-permitted act, so he guarantees accordingly, which is half. So much so that if the circumciser cuts the glans and the circumcised person recovers, full blood money is obligatory upon him, because the excess is the glans, which is a complete organ, so full blood money is obligatory upon him. And if he dies, half blood money is obligatory due to what we mentioned, and this is among the rarest and most strange issues, where more is obligatory upon recovery and less upon death. Thus in al-Tabyeen. And if it is said: This contradicts all issues of blood money, because whenever the effect of a transgression increases, its liability decreases. The answer: Because Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said in al-Nawadir: Indeed, when he recovered, the guarantee of the glans is upon him, and it is an intended organ with no equal in the person, so its substitute is estimated by the substitute of the person, just as in cutting the tongue. And if he dies, then the destruction of the person occurred by two acts: one permitted (cutting the foreskin) and the other not permitted (cutting the glans), so he is liable for half the value of the person. And if it is said: The half in the substitute depends on equality in the cause, and it is absent, because cutting the glans is inevitably more conducive to destruction than cutting the foreskin, so it is like cutting the hand with a neck incision. The answer: Because each one is potentially destructive and potentially not, and the difference is not measurable, unlike the neck incision which cannot be non-destructive. Thus in al-Inaya.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage discusses liability of medical practitioners (cupper, veterinarian, phlebotomist) within the context of ijarah. They are not liable if they do not exceed the customary limits because their action is obligatory under the contract, and obligatory actions do not coexist with liability. However, if they can avoid harm (like beating cloth) and they fail to do so, they are liable. But for phlebotomy, the extent of harm depends on internal strength, unknown to the practitioner, so they are not liable unless they exceed the norm. Then an unusual case: if a circumciser (khitan) cuts the glans (hashafa) and the patient recovers, the full diya (blood money) is due because the glans is a complete organ. If the patient dies, half diya is due because death was caused by two actions: one permitted (cutting the foreskin) and one not (cutting the glans). This is a rare case where more is due upon recovery than upon death. The proof is from Imam Muhammad in Al-Nawadir, and the reasoning is that the glans has no equal except the whole person, so its value is estimated by the value of the person, like the tongue. When death occurs, the two actions share the causation, so liability is halved. An objection that the glans cut is more likely fatal than foreskin cut is rejected because both actions are potentially fatal, but the difference is not precise, unlike cutting the neck.

Key Terms
حجام (ḥajjām): A cupper; one who performs hijama.
بزاغ (bazzāgh): A veterinarian, specifically for animals.
فصاد (faṣṣād): A phlebotomist; one who performs bloodletting (fasd).
ختان (khitān): Circumciser.
حشفة (ḥashafah): The glans of the penis.
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation for homicide or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the glans is worth half the person.
B) Because recovery indicates the cut was effective, death indicates shared causation.
C) Because the circumciser intended to cause harm.
D) Because the patient's life is less valuable.

Key Takeaway
A medical practitioner is not liable within customary limits because the act is obligatory; the circumciser is a rare exception where full diya is due upon recovery of the patient due to the glans being a complete organ with no second.

Liability of a Porter for a Broken Jar: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Providing the ruling on liability and options when a jar breaks during hire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on porter liability when a jar breaks in ijarah and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A porter's liability depends on whether goods are trust (Imam) or guaranteed (Sahibayn) – the thamarah affects the owner's recourse.

This passage examines a classic scenario in ijarah: a porter carrying a jar along the Euphrates route. The jar breaks. Who bears the loss? The answer differs within the Hanafi school based on the nature of the hireling's responsibility. Let us dissect the two scenarios and the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between breakage caused by the porter's own action (slipping/stumbling) and breakage caused by external force (crowd shoving).
2
State the owner's options in the first scenario
When the porter breaks the jar by his act, the owner has two options: claim value at loading place (no hire) or claim value at breakage place (pay pro rata hire).
Both Imam and Sahibayn agree on this scenario.
3
Identify the dispute in the second scenario
When the breakage is due to external force, Imam Abu Hanifa holds the porter is not liable (goods are trust), while the Sahibayn hold the porter is liable (common hireling is guarantor).
4
Compare the underlying usul
Imam views the ajir mushtarak as an amin (trustee) until delivery; Sahibayn view him as a damin (guarantor) because he is hired for the work.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Imam, owner bears loss; under Sahibayn, porter pays value at breakage place and receives full hire, with no option for the owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : فإن انكسر...الخ ; يعني فلو انكسر دنّ في طريق الفرات، فللمالك أن يضمن الحمّال قيمة ذلك الدنّ التي تقوم في مكان حمله ، ولا أجر له، أو ضَمِنَ قيمته في مكان كسره ، وللحمال الأجر بحسابه. وهذا إذا كان الكسرُ بصنعه بأن زَلِق أو عَثِر ، وإن كان من غير صنعه بأن زحمه الناس فانكسر فلا يضمن عند الإمام ; لأنَّ المتاع أمانة عنده ، وعندهما يضمن قيمته في موضع انكسر ; لأنه سليم العمل بإيصاله بملكه، فيعطيه أجره، ولا يخير في هذه الصورة عندهما ; لأن العين مضمونة على الأجير المشترك عندهما. كما صرحوا به.
[1] His saying: 'if a jar breaks...' etc.; i.e., if a jar breaks on the Euphrates route, the owner may either hold the porter liable for the value of that jar as assessed at the place of loading, and he has no hire, or he may hold him liable for its value at the place of breakage, and the porter receives his hire proportionally. This is if the break is due to his action, such as slipping or stumbling. But if it is not due to his action, such as people crowding him and it breaks, then he is not liable according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the goods are a trust with him. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is liable for its value at the place of breakage, because he performed the work soundly by delivering it to his possession, so he gives him his hire, and he is not given an option in this case according to them, because the item is guaranteed upon the common hireling in their view. As they have stated explicitly.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage deals with a jar being broken by a porter (hammal) in the course of hire. The owner has the option: either claim the value of the jar at the place of loading (and forfeit the hire), or claim the value at the place of breakage (and pay the porter pro rata hire). This applies if the break occurs due to the porter's action (e.g., slipping or stumbling). If it occurs due to external force (e.g., crowd), then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the porter is not liable because the goods are a trust (amanah). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the porter is liable and must pay the value at the place of breakage, plus he gets his full hire, and the owner does not have the option in this scenario. This is because the common hireling (ajir mushtarak) is a guarantor (damin) of the item in their view.

Key Terms
دن (dann): A jar or container for liquids.
حمال (ḥammāl): A porter; a hireling who carries goods.
أجير مشترك (ajīr mushtarak): A hireling who works for multiple parties, not exclusively for one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a jar breaks due to the porter's own action (slipping), the owner has two options. Which of the following is one of them?
A) Claim full value at breakage place and give no hire.
B) Claim value at loading place and forfeit hire.
C) Claim value at loading place and still pay hire.
D) Claim double the value.

Key Takeaway
The liability of a porter for broken goods in Hanafi law depends on the cause of breakage and, in external force, on whether the hireling is considered a trustee or guarantor.
